<span id="freeTextContainer15665827512319509964">I really wanted to enjoy this book and whilst I appreciate that it is written very well in a literary sense, it did not appeal to me at all. The relentless misandry made it quite a boring read, despite its short length. I could see flashes of brilliance in this book (the dancing princesses, the character of dog-woman), however none of it was fleshed out to any sort of degree to make me want to read on. All in all a difficult 140 pages to trawl through. I can't help thinking that if it were a lit</span>
  <span id="freeText15665827512319509964" style="display:none">I really wanted to enjoy this book and whilst I appreciate that it is written very well in a literary sense, it did not appeal to me at all. The relentless misandry made it quite a boring read, despite its short length. I could see flashes of brilliance in this book (the dancing princesses, the character of dog-woman), however none of it was fleshed out to any sort of degree to make me want to read on. All in all a difficult 140 pages to trawl through. I can't help thinking that if it were a little longer and the relentless male bashing toned down I'd have enjoyed it more, but then that wasn't probably the authors aim.</span>

    <a title="tee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/789575-tee"><img alt="tee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1389318091p2/789575.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12647219?book_show_action=true">Jan 16, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="tee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="tee" href="/user/show/789575-tee">tee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12647219" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5520841708486213537">Wah. Some of Winterson's works make me feel as if I completely missing out on something, like it's going straight over my head. Which is likely the case considering I am not the most intellectual of sorts but I don't like being reminded of this when trying to enjoy a novel. Further, with most books that are a little too 'smart' for me, I usually understand why. Either it's the content, or the heavy vocabulary or some such thing. <br/><br/>But Winterson ... sometimes I feel like <i>I just don't get it</i>. Rather</span>
  <span id="freeText5520841708486213537" style="display:none">Wah. Some of Winterson's works make me feel as if I completely missing out on something, like it's going straight over my head. Which is likely the case considering I am not the most intellectual of sorts but I don't like being reminded of this when trying to enjoy a novel. Further, with most books that are a little too 'smart' for me, I usually understand why. Either it's the content, or the heavy vocabulary or some such thing. <br /><br />But Winterson ... sometimes I feel like <i>I just don't get it</i>. Rather like some people wouldn't understand some of my coded journal entries, like when I'm waffling about something that makes sense to me because it's in my head and I created it, but anyone else- good luck.<br /><br />I liked parts of this book. I was disinterested in the majority of it. I couldn't have cared less if I hadn't picked it back up to finish it and only continued to the end out of duty. I was a little perplexed as to why this book seems to be so highly rated by others on goodreads. <br /><br />It was worth it for the parts that I <i>did</i> like though. I enjoyed the play with time and gravity (and so on). The prose was true Winterson, deep and rich. The imagery was stunning and her ideas - completely fantastical. I was swept away by certain paragraphs, feeling as if I could almost smell and taste her descriptions.<br /><br />I'd like to re-read it and try and get my head around the rest, but there's so many other books that I'd actually love to read. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5520841708486213537" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2361924997" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2361924997" />
    <a title="Joshie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11973316-joshie"><img alt="Joshie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588954776p2/11973316.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2361924997?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joshie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joshie" href="/user/show/11973316-joshie">Joshie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/15827386-sexing-the-cherry">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=magical-realism">magical-realism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=2-star">2-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11973316-joshie?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2361924997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11627847419012446745">Surprisingly, this was too strange and disjointed for my taste. Packed with odd sexual encounters (although not as pornographic as the title suggested) and murders contradicting some of its religious themes and tone of justice, the inclusion of time and love as concepts did not seem enough to tie everything Winterson was trying to say in this novel of attachments, outcast characters, known mythologies and fragments of histories reimagined with reflective feminism to boot. Still, I will not deny </span>
  <span id="freeText11627847419012446745" style="display:none">Surprisingly, this was too strange and disjointed for my taste. Packed with odd sexual encounters (although not as pornographic as the title suggested) and murders contradicting some of its religious themes and tone of justice, the inclusion of time and love as concepts did not seem enough to tie everything Winterson was trying to say in this novel of attachments, outcast characters, known mythologies and fragments of histories reimagined with reflective feminism to boot. Still, I will not deny the sheer beauty of the tale of the 12 Dancing Princesses and how some phrases made me chuckle. Moreover, I also can't ignore the fact that Winterson has used the phrase <i>"Time is a great deadener."</i> thrice in three different novels — spare me with this glaring reality some time. Its initial impact has been lost.<br /><br />First Winterson book that disappointed me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11627847419012446745" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1136620664741851677">I didn't get on with this, too schematic for me. Winterson's introduction was interesting and she makes the point that the book was mostly about the space between things. But I had the same problem with this as I do with most poetry in that I couldn't make sense of all the disparate elements</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hIc8p3CpQlajfwR60R3SGEQkSoqL6/jvdDiwGUY5BcjxGk6sgBDSN2Ida+m2qEJgDKpeGY9bcYfhFByso6jPeA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

In Urdu, basti means any space, from the most intimate to the most universal, in which groups of people come together to try to live together, and the universal question at the heart of the book is how to constitute a common world. What brings people together? What tears them apart? “When the world was still all new, when the sky was fresh and the earth not yet soiled, when trees breathed through centuries and ages spoke around in the voices of birds, how astonished he was that everything was so new and yet looked so old”—so the book begins, with a mythic, even mystic, vision of harmony, as the hero, Zakir, looks back on his childhood in a subcontinent that had not yet been divided between Muslims and Hindus. But Zakir is abruptly evicted from this paradise—real or imagined—into the maelstrom of history. The new country of Pakistan is born, separating him once and for all from the woman he loves, and in a jagged and jarring sequence of scenes we witness a nation and a psyche torn into existence only to be torn apart again and again by political, religious, economic, linguistic, personal, and sexual conflicts—in effect, a world of loneliness. Zakir, whose name means “remember,” serves as the historian of this troubled place, while the ties he maintains across the years with old friends—friends who run into one another in cafés and on corners and the odd other places where history takes a time-out—suggest that the possibility of reconciliation is not simply a dream. The characters wait for a sign that minds and hearts may still meet. He recited it to her by the hour and she ached, raged and chafed under it because his eloquence denied her language.&quot; &quot;The Kiss&quot; takes the traditional story of Tamburlaine\'s wife and gives it a new and refreshing ending. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer8221673710645733867">Novelist Jeanette Winterson was born in Manchester, England in 1959. She was adopted and brought up in Accrington, Lancashire, in the north of England. Her strict Pentecostal Evangelist upbringing provides the background to her acclaimed first novel, <i>Oranges Are Not the Only Fruit</i>, published in 1985. She graduated from St Catherine's College, Oxford, and moved to London where she worked as an assi</span>
  <span id="freeText8221673710645733867" style="display:none">Novelist Jeanette Winterson was born in Manchester, England in 1959. She was adopted and brought up in Accrington, Lancashire, in the north of England. Her strict Pentecostal Evangelist upbringing provides the background to her acclaimed first novel, <i>Oranges Are Not the Only Fruit</i>, published in 1985. She graduated from St Catherine's College, Oxford, and moved to London where she worked as an assistant editor at Pandora Press.<br /><br />One of the most original voices in British fiction to emerge during the 1980s, Winterson was named as one of the 20 "Best of Young British Writers" in a promotion run jointly between the literary magazine Granta and the Book Marketing Council. <br /><br />She adapted <i>Oranges Are Not the Only Fruit</i> for BBC television in 1990 and also wrote "Great Moments in Aviation," a television screenplay directed by Beeban Kidron for BBC2 in 1994. She is editor of a series of new editions of novels by Virginia Woolf published in the UK by Vintage. She is a regular contributor of reviews and articles to many newspapers and journals and has a regular column published in The Guardian. Her radio drama includes the play Text Message, broadcast by BBC Radio in November 2001. <br /><br />Winterson lives in Gloucestershire and London. Her work is published in 28 countries. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8221673710645733867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
